<h1 class="content__title blog__title">The 5 French Mother Sauces Every Cook Should&nbsp;Know</h1>

<div class="meta">

    <a aria-label="Pin this!" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250428024655/https://www.pinterest.com/pin/create/button/?url=https://food52.com/blog/12209-the-five-mother-sauces-every-cook-should-know&amp;media=https://images.food52.com/woDY1g15Qm3OiMIU4pDo9hhg-lU=/fit-in/1200x1200/b3d2dec7-34af-4f13-a165-17372f7543b8--2021-0302_hollandaise-sauce_3x2_james-ransom-057.jpg&amp;description=The%205%20French%20Mother%20Sauces%20Every%20Cook%20Should%20Know%20on%20Food52" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer" class="social__link social__link--pinterest" data-network="Pinterest" data-social-action="Pin" data-pin-custom="true" data-pin-do="none" data-pin-media="https://images.food52.com/woDY1g15Qm3OiMIU4pDo9hhg-lU=/fit-in/1200x1200/b3d2dec7-34af-4f13-a165-17372f7543b8--2021-0302_hollandaise-sauce_3x2_james-ransom-057.jpg" data-pin-url="https://food52.com/blog/12209-the-five-mother-sauces-every-cook-should-know" data-pin-description="The 5 French Mother Sauces Every Cook Should Know on Food52" data-ga-event="inPageInteraction" data-ga-event-action="Pinterest Share" data-ga-event-label="Below Article Hero Image" data-ga-destination-url="none">

    </a>
  </li>

<li>
    <a aria-label="Email to a friend" href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/cdn-cgi/l/email-protection#3c034f495e56595f4801685459190e0c09190e0c7a4e59525f54190e0c71534854594e190e0c6f5d495f594f190e0c794a594e45190e0c7f535357190e0c6f5453495058190e0c7752534b190e0c5352190e0c7a535358090e1a5d514c075e53584501751c48545552571c4553491a1f0f050750501c505557591c4854554f06190c78190c7d190c78190c7d5448484c4f190f7d190e7a190e7a5a535358090e125f5351190e7a5e50535b190e7a0d0e0e0c0511485459115a554a591151534854594e114f5d495f594f11594a594e45115f535357114f5453495058115752534b" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer" class="social__link social__link--email" data-network="Email" data-social-action="Share" data-ga-event-label="Below Article Hero Image" data-ga-event="inPageInteraction" data-ga-event-action="Email Share" data-ga-destination-url="none">
      <svg aria-hidden="true" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" viewbox="0 0 17.1 12.5"><path d="M5.9 6.4L.6 12.5h16l-5.3-6.1-2.5 2.4c-.1.2-.3.2-.5 0L5.9 6.4zM17.1.6l-5.3 5.2 5.3 6.1V.6zM0 .6v11.3l5.3-6.1L0 .6zM.6 0l7.9 7.8 8-7.8H.6z" fill="currentColor"/></svg>
    </a>
  </li>
</ul>

</div>

<div id="publisher_div" data-jw-placement-id="6XvmbR2u"> 
        </div>

<div class="content__text-container">
          <div class="content__text blog__text js-blog-text js-subnav-content clearfix blog__text--legacy">
              <p><em>The five French mother sauces are: Béchamel, Velouté, Espagnole, Hollandaise, and Tomato. Read on to learn how to make each one.</em></p>
              <hr>
              <p><strong>In the 19th century, Marie-Antoine Carême anointed Béchamel, Velouté, Espagnole, and tomato sauce</strong> as the building blocks for all other sauces in his work <em><a href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250428024655/http://www.amazon.com/gp/reader/0543991261" target="_blank">L'Art de la Cuisine Française au Dix-Neuvième Siecle</a></em>. Later on, Hollandaise got added to the family. Since then, many people consider others sauces—sweet and savory from all around the world—as unofficial extended relatives of these five sauces.</p><div fsnoad="true" class="ad-slot ad-slot--mobile-inline" id="inlineArticle"></div>
              <p>Though some will argue for the importance of <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/17349-chimichurri-lamb-chops">chimichurri</a> and <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/blog/9335-spur-of-the-moment-dark-chocolate-sauce-without-a-recipe">chocolate sauce</a>, it's a knowledge of the five French mother sauces that will prove essential. They may seem intimidating, but mother sauces will nurture your kitchen confidence. With a few simple ingredients (mostly flour, butter, and a liquid) and a couple easy techniques, these five sauces, all equally important to your cooking repertoire, serve as the starting point for a slew of other classics.</p><div class="product-well"><div class="product-well__label">Shop the Story</div><div id="relatedProductsRoot" data-show-article-cta="true"><div class="product-well__carousel-spacer"></div></div></div>
              <p>Once you get the feel for these sauces, you’ll be able to whisk them up whenever you want to get fancy. And soon enough, you'll feel confident enough to break tradition and take that Mother Sauce somewhere she’s never gone before. <strong>Here's what you need to know about the building blocks of sauces:</strong></p>
              <p><a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/blog/8828-how-to-make-a-roux"><img src="http://web.archive.org/web/20250428024655im_/https://images.food52.com/ah0AT28Rd6ZgS3tQyGauhRcE1S4=/3dcef38c-5bbc-4aec-a06d-46377cc962c5--15808541673_0106386d99_b.jpg" alt="" width="49%"></a>  <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/blog/8828-how-to-make-a-roux"><img src="http://web.archive.org/web/20250428024655im_/https://images.food52.com/R-Cxu172qzJHnyMfQMGljzy0wm0=/ffc683e9-cc59-46d3-84a3-5ab04551b87f--16241173400_fff2c1ec27_b.jpg" alt="" width="49%"></a></p><div fsnoad="true" class="ad-slot ad-slot--mobile-inline" id="inlineArticleTwo"></div>
              <p>Beyond flavor, the most important element of any sauce is its ability to smother and cling to whatever it gets drizzled, dolloped, or poured on. That means making the sauce thick and stable, which is accomplished with three techniques: a <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/blog/8828-how-to-make-a-roux">roux</a>, an <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/blog/10150-all-about-emulsification">emulsifier</a>, and a reduction (liquid that's slowly cooked down until thick). </p>
              <p>Four out of the five mother sauces start with a roux. Roux is a fancy name for flour mixed with fat. Equal parts butter and flour get cooked over medium heat, then a liquid gets added. This mixture then boils, thickens (reduces), and becomes the base of your sauce. Just note, if you’re making a white sauce—like Béchamel or Velouté—do not brown the butter, as it will darken the finished product. The last mother sauce is a product of emulsification, which I'll explain below. </p>
              <p><strong>More: Is it your first time making a roux? <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/blog/8828-how-to-make-a-roux">Here's how to do it</a>, step by step.</strong></p>
              <p><a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/blog/8828-how-to-make-a-roux"><img src="http://web.archive.org/web/20250428024655im_/https://images.food52.com/aWE6a4hNjpPp8G87jccVhcEnu8Y=/ec925c97-091b-4a58-a876-765f5e017c31--16242676707_645eac0670_b.jpg" alt="" width="753"></a></p>
              <p><strong>Here are the basic formulas of the five mother sauces:</strong></p>
              <p><strong><em><a href="#h_30077075191561416004351">Béchamel</a>:</em></strong> Roux + Dairy (traditionally milk or cream)<br><strong><em><a href="#h_418843424161561416009665">Velouté</a>:</em></strong> Roux + White Stock (traditionally <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/blog/9739-how-to-make-chicken-stock-without-a-recipe">chicken</a>, but also <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/blog/10119-how-to-make-vegetable-stock-without-a-recipe">vegetable</a> or fish)<br><strong><em><a href="#h_561310595211561416014570">Espagnole</a>:</em></strong> Roux + Brown Stock (traditionally veal or <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/blog/1395-beef-stock">beef</a>)<br><strong><em><a href="#h_451031616241561416019631">Tomato</a>:</em></strong> Roux + Tomatoes (or, go the Italian route by skipping the roux and simply reducing tomatoes over medium-low heat until thick)<br><em><a href="#h_588433728101561415943978"><strong>Hollandaise</strong></a><strong>:</strong> </em>Egg Yolks + Clarified Melted Butter + Acid (like lemon juice or white wine)</p>
              <p><img src="http://web.archive.org/web/20250428024655im_/https://images.food52.com/HXu9qGzfIivMyDCnCfDYzalX2_k=/0f4fb8da-6f33-4f67-8f12-ed2eb21cfe07--16241169650_989b8c8d45_b.jpg" alt="" width="753"></p>
              <p>Now that you understand the basics, <strong>let’s talk about each mother sauce in more detail (and what to pair them with):</strong></p>
              <h1 id="h_30077075191561416004351">1. Béchamel</h1>
              <p>If you’ve eaten homemade <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/blog/11351-how-to-make-macaroni-and-cheese-without-a-recipe">macaroni and cheese</a>, a classic <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/17203-the-green-madame">croque madame</a>, or <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/20687-birthday-lasagna">lasagna</a>, chances are you’ve experienced <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/2759-bechamel-sauce">the rich creaminess of Béchamel</a>. It can be made in its most basic form by just combining roux and cream, or it can be mixed with other ingredients to create new sauces: Mornay is made by adding Gruyère or Parmesan, and mustard sauce is made by adding—you guessed it—mustard.</p>
              <p><a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/25114-trent-pierce-s-miso-creamed-kale"><img src="http://web.archive.org/web/20250428024655im_/https://images.food52.com/BUESpk8jUfR2Gzj8JSLGM8XniwA=/75472005-2544-40fb-954a-8f066018b6ab--2013-0611_genius-miso-creamed-kale-312.jpg" alt="" width="753"></a></p>
              <h3><strong>Recipes to Flaunt Your Béchamel Skills</strong></h3>
              <p><a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/81957-creamiest-baked-mac-and-cheese"><strong>Creamiest Baked Mac &amp; Cheese</strong></a></p>
              <p>Baking mac &amp; cheese means you get the best of both worlds: a crunchy topping and a creamy interior. Recipe developer Josh Cohen has a trick for avoiding any dryness during baking: undercook the pasta and use extra cheese sauce.</p>
              <p><a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/76952-croque-madame-hot-dish"><strong>Croque Madame Hot Dish</strong></a></p>
              <p>The classic ham-and-cheesy croque monsieur becomes a madame with the addition of an egg. In this recipe from Justine Chapple’s cookbook <em>Just Cook It</em>, it becomes a hot dish, a Midwestern casserole layered with béchamel, ham, Gruyère, and topped with fried eggs.</p>
              <p><a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/77779-lasagna-bolognese"><strong>Lasagna Bolognese</strong></a></p>
              <p>A proper lasagna bolognese requires time. Time to make the meat sauce; time to make the parmigiano-leaden béchamel; time to layer the noodles, sauces, and cheese; time to give it a nice long bake; and, most importantly, time to savor every bite.</p>
              <p><strong>Here are some other ways to use Béchamel:</strong></p>
              <ul>
<li>Swap in Béchamel for some of the cream in a <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/32429-lacinato-kale-gratin">gratin</a>.</li>
<li>Pour it over <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/2073-griddled-polenta-cakes-with-caramelized-onions-goat-cheese-and-honey">polenta cakes</a> and broil for a few minutes until bubbly and golden brown.</li>
</ul>
              <div style="position: relative; overflow: hidden; padding-bottom: 56.25%;"><iframe src="http://web.archive.org/web/20250428024655if_/https://cdn.jwplayer.com/players/12g6WMXh-eXbSrbZM.html" style="position: absolute;" width="100%" height="100%" scrolling="auto" frameborder="0"></iframe></div>
              <p><em>Once you've mastered basic Béchamel, here's how to get more creative:</em> Spike a classic Béchamel with soy and miso for a new take on <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/25114-trent-pierce-s-miso-creamed-kale">Trent Pierce’s Miso-Creamed Kale</a> or <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/17768-nobu-s-fried-asparagus-with-miso-dressing">Nobu’s Fried Asparagus</a>.</p>
              <h1 id="h_418843424161561416009665">2. Velouté</h1>
              <p>Like good old Béchamel, Velouté begins with a white roux, but then it gets mixed with white stock made from fish, chicken, or veal. Technically not a finished sauce, it's used as a flavorful starting point for <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/31765-chicken-giblet-gravy">gravies</a>, mushroom sauces (hello <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/31905-mini-chicken-pot-pies">chicken pot pie</a>), and shrimp sauce (hello <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/19180-roasted-cantaloupe-and-shrimp-bisque">shrimp bisque</a>). </p>
              <p><a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/7483-vegetarian-mushroom-thyme-gravy"><img src="http://web.archive.org/web/20250428024655im_/https://images.food52.com/2vAgxQLYQuoqsof7nVqv9xgPifM=/cf2bd075-df34-4c11-a982-5c777c033ee4--2014-1030_suite-one-studio_gravy-boat_mid-015.jpg" alt="" width="753"></a></p>
              <h3>Get Your Velouté On With These Recipes</h3>
              <p><strong><a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/928-veloute-aux-champignons-de-paris-button-mushroom-veloute">Velouté aux Champignons de Paris (Button Mushroom Soup)</a></strong></p>
              <p>Made with plenty of butter and cognac, this Parisian-inspired mushroom soup is just as good hot as it is cold.</p>
              <p><a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/39193-deep-dish-chicken-pot-pie"><strong>Deep-Dish Chicken Pot Pie</strong></a></p>
              <p>Is there anything cozier than chicken pot pie? Yep: deep-dish chicken pot pie. This creamy meal is baked in a springform pan, making a hefty pie that’ll really stick to your ribs.</p>
              <p><a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/86878-buttermilk-drop-biscuits-and-sausage-gravy-recipe"><strong>Buttermilk Drop Biscuits &amp; Sausage Gravy From Millie Peartree</strong></a></p>
              <p>Chef Millie Peartree’s biscuits and sausage gravy are for those lazy mornings when you’re craving a breakfast that’ll keep you full for hours.</p>
              <p><strong>Here are some other ways to use </strong><strong>Velouté:</strong></p>
              <ul>
<li>Whip up Velouté with veal stock, then use it to make <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/32535-old-school-swedish-meatballs">Swedish Meatballs</a>.</li>
<li>Smother <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/8240-buttermilk-biscuits-with-sausage-gravy">biscuits</a> with an herby gravy for breakfast.</li>
</ul>
              <p><em>Once you've mastered basic Velouté, here's how to get more creative: </em>Make velouté vegetarian with a mushroom-based stock for this <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/7483-vegetarian-mushroom-thyme-gravy">Vegetarian Mushroom Thyme Gravy</a>.</p>
              <h1 id="h_561310595211561416014570">3. Espagnole</h1>
              <p>Although some think blond roux have more fun, Espagnole proves that dark roux know how to party, too. Also known as brown sauce, Espagnole begins with a mirepoix (carrots, celery, and onions), beef stock, and deglazed brown bits (fond) from beef bones. From there, tomato paste and spices may be added.</p>
              <p>To make a demi-glace, a rich French brown sauce, combine the Espagnole with more beef stock; to create Bordelaise, a red wine sauce that pairs well with steak and mushrooms, mix the demi-glace with red wine and herbs. Serve this with <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/18568-steven-raichlen-s-salt-crusted-beef-tenderloin-grilled-in-cloth-lomo-al-trapo">filet mignon</a> for an excellent dinner.</p>
              <p><a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/9111-dan-barber-s-braised-short-ribs"><img src="http://web.archive.org/web/20250428024655im_/https://images.food52.com/GEf1_gMnUiAakgfjGntxC5cgb2Y=/73f53463-7999-4698-893b-d16f7dc2b0bf--5349824048_0f7d18165a_z.jpg" alt="" width="753"></a></p>
              <h3><strong>Explore Espagnole In These Recipes</strong></h3>
              <p><a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/82038-chopped-steak-recipe-with-red-wine-sauce%20"><strong>Chopped Steak Marchand de Vin</strong></a></p>
              <p>This recipe, from <em>Canal House: Cook Something</em>, calls for store-bought veal demi-glace—we’re a fan of any shortcut that makes dinner faster.</p>
              <p><a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/9111-dan-barber-s-braised-short-ribs%20"><strong>Dan Barber's Braised Short Ribs</strong></a></p>
              <p>Chef Dan Barber’s braised short ribs take classic Espagnole to new heights with the addition of bright tamarind paste, which cuts through the fattiness of the meat.</p>
              <p><strong>Here are some other ways to use</strong><strong> Espagnole and its variations:</strong></p>
              <ul>
<li>Pair <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/14721-pomegranate-roast-lamb">roast lamb</a> with a demi-glace.</li>
<li>Drizzle a little Bordelaise over <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/3677-mushroom-risotto-with-creamy-leeks">mushroom risotto</a>.</li>
</ul>
              <h1 id="h_451031616241561416019631">4. Tomato</h1>
              <p>Probably the first mother sauce you ever tasted (over a heaping <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/22467-rao-s-meatballs">bowl of spaghetti</a>), tomato sauce is often a mixture of just onions, garlic, and tomatoes. Although some traditionalists may start with a roux, most tomato sauces merely rely on a tomato reduction to build flavor and create thickness.</p>
              <p><a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/13722-marcella-hazan-s-tomato-sauce-with-onion-and-butter"><img src="http://web.archive.org/web/20250428024655im_/https://images.food52.com/IvRc9yLGdr1oUo_EBgZDm7NPK8k=/f63af57f-f992-46f4-94ec-d8e60196e761--mD_QkYwXOrBhdIML4Ku5nW2MHd702_YP-5_N51OWSxU.jpg" alt="" width="753"></a></p>
              <h3><strong>Tomato, Tom-ah-to—You Need These Sauce Recipes</strong></h3>
              <p><a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/85411-best-pizza-sauce-recipe"><strong>Pizza Sauce</strong></a></p>
              <p>Pizza sauce certainly isn’t classic French, but the two are no doubt linked. We like to keep a quart of this in the freezer for pizza-emergencies.</p>
              <p><strong><a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/65845-yotam-ottolenghi-s-shakshuka">Yotam Ottolenghi's Shakshuka</a></strong></p>
              <p>Shakshuka’s saucy tomato base isn't traditionally French either, but it <em>is</em> perfect for savory breakfast fans—but we like this recipe any time of day.</p>
              <p><em>Once you've mastered basic tomato sauce, here's how to get more creative:</em> <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/13722-marcella-hazan-s-tomato-sauce-with-onion-butter" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Marcella Hazan's tomato sauce</a> can't be beat, but if you want to think outside of Italy, use your tomato sauce to make <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/17991-lentil-cakes-tikka-masala">Lentil Cakes with Tikka Masala</a> instead. </p>
              <h1 id="h_588433728101561415943978">5. Hollandaise</h1>
              <p>Think of Hollandaise as a fancy mayonnaise that uses clarified butter in place of oil and gets drizzled over <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/21539-roasted-asparagus-with-poached-egg-and-lemon-mustard-sauce">asparagus</a> and eggs without judgment. Instead of using a roux or a reduction, Hollandaise uses the method of <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/blog/10150-all-about-emulsification">emulsification</a>: the act of using a binding agent (in this case, an egg yolk) to force two ingredients that don't mix well together (here, butter and lemon juice) to like each other immensely. Hollandaise takes patience, as you'll need to temper the mixture so that the eggs do not curdle. The sauce can break easily, but you can patch things back together by adding a little heavy cream and whisking until the sauce returns to its smooth state; or use Amanda's trick for <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/blog/3711-how-to-fix-broken-aioli">fixing broken aioli</a>—a close relative of Hollandaise sauce—by using the <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/blog/14876-kitchen-rescue-how-to-fix-a-broken-or-curdled-sauce">broken emulsification</a> to start your next batch. Sound like a lot of hard work? This <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/2699-fried-green-tomatoes">Fried Green Tomato Benedict</a> makes it all worth the trouble.</p>
              <p>When mixed with unsweetened whipped cream, Hollandaise suddenly becomes airy Mousseline that can be poured over <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/13407-le-bernardin-s-crispy-skinned-fish">fish</a> or <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/23272-penelope-casa-s-garlic-green-beans-judias-verdes-con-ajo">vegetables</a>. </p>
              <p><a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/30257-savory-oatmeal-with-ham-poached-eggs-and-hollandaise-sauce"><img src="http://web.archive.org/web/20250428024655im_/https://images.food52.com/izoRHjsHFxU453TDP_I3OT_1jTg=/ccce0526-1d87-45ab-9a33-691663487e7c--savory_oatmeal_1.jpg" alt="" width="753"></a></p>
              <h3>Recipes When You Want Hollandaise For Days</h3>
              <p><a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/85340-hollandaise-sauce-recipe"><strong>Hollandaise Sauce</strong></a></p>
              <p>Start off strong with a classic Hollandaise sauce recipe to keep bookmarked.</p>
              <p><a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/77154-avocado-toast-benedict"><strong>Avocado Toast Eggs Benedict</strong></a></p>
              <p>Eggs Benedict (or Florentine) are instantly improved when merged with another breakfast favorite: avocado toast.</p>
              <p><strong><a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/82165-poached-eggs-recipe-with-miso-brown-butter-hollandaise">Poached Eggs With Miso-Brown Butter Hollandaise</a></strong></p>
              <p>Try Food52 Resident Mandy Lee’s miso and brown butter Hollandaise, and you may never make the classic recipe ever again.</p>
              <p><a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/77621-easier-bearnaise-sauce" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Béarnaise</a> is another close relative to Hollandaise. But unlike Hollandaise, which has lemon juice in it, Béarnaise is perfumed with tarragon, shallots, and white wine vinegar.</p>
              <p><strong>Here are some other ways to use</strong><strong> Hollandaise:</strong></p>
              <ul>
<li>Drizzle it over <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/18201-brigit-binns-crab-cakes">crab cakes</a>, or use it as a dipping sauce.</li>
<li>Substitute it for mayo in <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/4734-garlicky-roasted-potato-salad" target="_blank" rel="noopener">roasted potato salad</a>.</li>
</ul>
              <p><em>Once you've mastered basic Hollandaise, here's how to get more creative:</em> Let another breakfast staple enjoy the creaminess of Hollandaise with this <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/30257-savory-oatmeal-with-ham-poached-eggs-and-hollandaise-sauce">Savory Oatmeal</a> recipe. Or take your next <a href="/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/recipes/4817-caramelized-pork-banh-mi">Caramelized Pork Bahn Mi</a> to new heights by replacing the mayonnaise with a Sriracha-spiked Hollandaise sauce. </p>
              <p><em>Short rib photo by Sarah Shatz; last photo by Marta Greber; all others by James Ransom. This article originally ran in February of 2015; we're re-running it now to revisit the classics—and just in case you don't know them yet.</em></p>
          </div>

<div class="author__bio">
      In January of 2004, I received a diagnosis that changed my life. I was diagnosed with Lupus, an autoimmune disease that in my case attacked my kidneys and brain. Due to the intensity of the initial “flare up” of the disease, I became renal insufficient and eventually faced kidney failure. Amazingly, through great medicine, wonderful family and friends, and an enormous amount of support, I became stronger and healthier and miraculously, my kidneys partially regenerated. I no longer depend on dialysis and by regulating my diet, I depend on fewer medications. Five years later, I work part time and live a full and utterly enjoyable life. My dietary restrictions have transformed into a real passion for food and I hope to be able to pass along my favorite finds to others facing similar dietary challenges. Be creative, be friendly, and be full!
    </div>
</div>

</div>
      </div>

<div class="comments__body">Carême's FOUR "Mother Sauces" were Allemande,  Velouté, Espagnol, et Béchamel.  Escoffier demoted Sauce Allemande to be a lesser Velouté and added Sauce Hollandaise et Tomate.  I often also consider a vinaigrette a sauce because there are so many variations today, both hot and cold and if you you can't make a good vinaigrette or balsamic vinaigrette you shouldn't be bragging about how much better you are than an experienced Saucier de la Cuisine.  I have worked and lived with famous Internationally known Chefs and Restaurateurs and they NEVER even once insinuated they were better than another Chef de Cuisine.  Pffff.....ridicule !!  </div>

<div class="comments__body">You should actually watch the video instead of simply dismissing it out of hand, because you just made yourself look like a complete fool. <br/><br/>In the video, Alex literally references Escoffier and shows where he himself says that Hollandaise is NOT a mother sauce. The idea that it is comes from a TRANSLATION of Escoffier's work which rearranged the sauces out of order. So the source for the claim that Hollandaise isn't a mother sauce isn't just "YouTube," it's Escoffier himself! You should look it up in the original French instead of relying on fundamentally flawed translations!<br/><br/>Every culinary arts curriculum that cites Escoffier to say that Hollandaise is a mother sauce is simply wrong. The 5th mother sauce is not Hollandaise, it's MAYONNAISE! If that was marked wrong on any exam, anywhere, it should be contested with a reference to the original source. The record needs to be set straight!</div>

<div class="comments__comment">

<div class="comments__main">
      <div class="comments__avatar avatar avatar--responsive-medium">
        <a href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/users/1222586-beth" title="Beth">
            <div style="max-width: 100%; width: 60px;"><div style="height: 0; padding-bottom: 100.0%; position: relative;"><img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" alt="Beth" class="avatar avatar--responsive-medium lazyload" width="60" height="60" style="width: 100%; height: 100%; position: absolute; top: 0; left: 0; display: block;" data-src="http://web.archive.org/web/20250428024655/https://images.food52.com/iV1Og0UUhsHlzQIooC9NNvbiGyQ=/124x124/b3cf7c6a-322f-471b-a0b2-fa7f12f43498--default-avatar.png"/></div></div>
        </a>
      </div>
      <div class="comments__content">
        <div class="comments__header">
          <a href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/users/1222586-beth" title="Beth">
            <span class="comments__username">Beth</span>
          </a>
          July 19, 2019
        </div>

<div class="comments__body">Couldn't be easier. Start with loose sausage, such as Jimmy Dean, break up and brown in frypan. Then add several tablespoons of flour - the usual ratio is 2 Tbls flour to l cup milk - and stir into the sausage in the pan. Don't drain the sausage, you want the fat for flavor and to thicken the sauce. Then add milk, little by little, stirring as you go. It will thicken pretty quickly and you really don't need any other seasoning. I use whole milk for more richness, but you can use 1 or 2% if you like. As soon as it is thick enough, pour over biscuits and enjoy!</div>

<div class="comments__action">&nbsp;</div>

</div>
    </div>
  </div>

        <a href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/users/1781297-john-bradley-heck" title="John B.">
            <div style="max-width: 100%; width: 60px;"><div style="height: 0; padding-bottom: 100.0%; position: relative;"><img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" alt="John B." class="avatar avatar--responsive-medium lazyload" width="60" height="60" style="width: 100%; height: 100%; position: absolute; top: 0; left: 0; display: block;" data-src="http://web.archive.org/web/20250428024655/https://images.food52.com/iV1Og0UUhsHlzQIooC9NNvbiGyQ=/124x124/b3cf7c6a-322f-471b-a0b2-fa7f12f43498--default-avatar.png"/></div></div>
        </a>
      </div>
      <div class="comments__content">
        <div class="comments__header">
          <a href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/users/1781297-john-bradley-heck" title="John B.">
            <span class="comments__username">John B.</span>
          </a>
          June 29, 2019
        </div>

<div class="comments__body">As a French trained Saucier of 38 years, I have always considered vinaigrette to be a mother sauce; some may scoff at this but here is my experience talking: A French vinaigrette is made with mustard, shallots, sugar, oil &amp; vinegar.A derivative of this is what is properly called a citronette---using citrus juice, ie, blood orange, grapefruit, pommelo, orange, lemon, or lime juice in place of vinegar. I make a Japanese version with tamari, yuzo citrus juice, garlic, sesame oil( sparingly), peanut oil and ginger. All vinaigrette can quickly be used as a marinade or as a drizzle sauce atop brochettes/kabobs, grilled meats...try herbes de Provence and red wine to flavor succulent grilled steaks and pork loin/tenderloin. Add vermouth and fresh chiffonade of basil for grilled seafood &amp; chicken breasts. Add mayonnaise to a vinaigrette for unctous potato salad or shrimp salad. In place of mayonnaise make tuna fish or egg salad. Use your imagination with vinaigrette/citronnete sauce &amp; come up with variable and healthy taste sensations.<br/> God bless all</div>

<div class="comments__action">&nbsp;</div>

<div class="comments__body">Sugar.  In my experience, no sugar -- ever -- in classic vinaigrette.  And IMO unless you're deliberately going for a sweet dressing, it doesn't belong in any savory salad.  An easy way to cut back on something that has become all too thoughtlessly included in so many products.  IMO.</div>

<div class="comments__body">I'm not going to contradict a saucier of that many years. Restaurants tend towards sweeter and saltier and richer than what we'd accept or consider at home. Any restaurant cole slaw recipe includes sugar and I never out it in mine at home because I can balance it out with the citrus from lemon rather than straight vinegar but the familiar at restaurants is the sweetness. When I make a citrus dressing I don't see the need for sugars  but I may consider honey so in the end, what's the difference? It's a sucrose in the mind and it's still about balance.</div>

<div class="comments__body">I have no problem contradicting a saucier of that many years not with MY judgment.  Whatever level is below amateur, that's me.  But I would contradict a saucier with Larousse Gastronomique, the 1000+ page bible of gastronomy. Pagee 998.  There is NO sugar, or honey, or fruit juice, in vinaigrette (nor mayonnaise).  Not even in any variation.  My preference, and no,  it's not authoritative, is that sugar ruins a savory salad. </div>

        <a href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/users/1327190-baroness" title="Baroness">
            <div style="max-width: 100%; width: 60px;"><div style="height: 0; padding-bottom: 100.0%; position: relative;"><img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAAAACH5BAEKAAEALAAAAAABAAEAAAICTAEAOw==" alt="Baroness" class="avatar avatar--responsive-medium lazyload" width="60" height="60" style="width: 100%; height: 100%; position: absolute; top: 0; left: 0; display: block;" data-src="http://web.archive.org/web/20250428024655/https://images.food52.com/iV1Og0UUhsHlzQIooC9NNvbiGyQ=/124x124/b3cf7c6a-322f-471b-a0b2-fa7f12f43498--default-avatar.png"/></div></div>

      </div>
      <div class="comments__content">
        <div class="comments__header">
          <a href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/users/1327190-baroness" title="Baroness">
            <span class="comments__username">Baroness</span>
          </a>
          March 4, 2019
        </div>

<div class="comments__body">A classic Roux, being French by birth, :) ALWAYS begins with butter---unsalted butter is best. While you can certainly create a Roux using almost any fat, it will definitely influence whatever you're putting it on/in &amp; could be heavier than you might like. There are 3 types of Roux: White, Blond, Brown. One certainly can use meat fat for a Brown Roux with a rich, hearty result. Provincial/country cooking often employs bacon fat for a Roux. There are plenty of recipes where even lard is used. The determining factor will be what you're doing with the sauce you're making, whether biscuit gravy or a Roux 'starter' for 4 of the 5 Mother Sauces which begin with a Roux. You can absolutely call it a "Roux" if it begins with fat or butter!  TIP: Use pastry or cake flour, which have a higher starch content &amp; will help your Roux have a happily thick beginning. Also, only use cold or room temp stock/liquid to make your Roux; using hot liquid will invariably cause lumps which are time-consuming to get out; in fact, you may not be able to get them all out.  Hoping this helps a wee bit. Happy Cooking~  :)</div>

<div class="comments__action">&nbsp;</div>

</div>
    </div>
  </div>

<div class="comments__body">Catherine de Medici, 1547 Queen of France, Italian<br/><br/>https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catherine_de%27_Medici<br/>-------------------<br/>I was gifted the book (link below) which covered a bit about her and some other killer, persons of history. <br/>--------<br/>https://www.amazon.com/Sprezzatura-Italian-Genius-Shaped-World/dp/038572019X/ref=asc_df_038572019X/?tag=hyprod-20&linkCode;=df0&hvadid;=312028586631&hvpos;=1o1&hvnetw;=g&hvrand;=15763562074899237097&hvpone;=&hvptwo;=&hvqmt;=&hvdev;=c&hvdvcmdl;=&hvlocint;=&hvlocphy;=9028903&hvtargid;=pla-334090922855&psc;=1<br/>----------<br/>Countries are not static populaces, nor did they all keep their borders closed. French cooking is more of right place right time, and the right influences of culture. All compounds are comprised of multiple elements. Cooking is a permutation process that just happened to be the perfect variables via observation/chance/experimentation in that this revision. Innate science done by many, made French cooking, it may have been born and refined in France, but that does not denote its ownership. Nor does it give credit to the compounded experience from the caveman cooking meat on a fire to the first roux.... <br/><br/>There is an innumerable amount of feats that allowed for a: spoon to stir a pan that was at a 400 degrees on the fire, that happened to have equal parts butter and flour.<br/><br/>Most things are as such, glory for the most recent plateau while we forget those before..... </div>

      </div>
      <div class="comments__content">
        <div class="comments__header">
          <a href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250428024655/https://food52.com/users/76975-scott-finkelstein-5" title="scott.finkelstein.5">
            <span class="comments__username">scott.finkelstein.5</span>
          </a>
          July 20, 2016
        </div>

<div class="comments__body">The French aren't the only ones to have developed the roux, puree, and emulsion methods of thickening. They actually occur in pretty much every cuisine, and the French specifically learned them from Italy. It also leaves out starch and gelatin thickened sauces popular worldwide.<br/><br/>Really, the mother sauces should be roux-thickened (bechamel, veloute), manual emulsions (mayo, hollandaise), boiled vegetables and purees (tomato, caramelized onions), natural emulsions (cream), gelatin (pan sauces), and starches (all the glossy/gloopy stuff using corn starch).</div>

<div class="comments__action">&nbsp;</div>

I make a Japanese version with tamari, yuzo citrus juice, garlic, sesame oil( sparingly), peanut oil and ginger. But, I wonder: here in the south most of my fat based rouxs start with bacon grease, not butter. Obviously, unless making a bechamell. But even even my Louisiana kin don't start a roux with butter, it would be shortening. We're still doing the same thing right? We just can't technically call it the same? \",\"imageSrcs\":null,\"createdAt\":\"2017-01-15T10:17:33.483-05:00\",\"helpfulCount\":5,\"authorComment\":false,\"isHighlighted\":false,\"childComments\":[{\"id\":413498,\"parentId\":335359,\"userId\":1327190,\"body\":\"A classic Roux, being French by birth, :) ALWAYS begins with butter---unsalted butter is best. While you can certainly create a Roux using almost any fat, it will definitely influence whatever you're putting it on/in \\u0026amp; could be heavier than you might like. There are 3 types of Roux: White, Blond, Brown. One certainly can use meat fat for a Brown Roux with a rich, hearty result. Provincial/country cooking often employs bacon fat for a Roux. There are plenty of recipes where even lard is used. The determining factor will be what you're doing with the sauce you're making, whether biscuit gravy or a Roux 'starter' for 4 of the 5 Mother Sauces which begin with a Roux. You can absolutely call it a \\\"Roux\\\" if it begins with fat or butter!  TIP: Use pastry or cake flour, which have a higher starch content \\u0026amp; will help your Roux have a happily thick beginning. Also, only use cold or room temp stock/liquid to make your Roux; using hot liquid will invariably cause lumps which are time-consuming to get out; in fact, you may not be able to get them all out.  Hoping this helps a wee bit.         (function() {
